RATING: 6
Mage Wars delivers exactly what you'd expect: two mages going toe to toe with plenty of asymmetrical spell casting goodness covering every type imaginable from enchantments, conjuration, slinging fire and lightning, traps, wands,  teleporting - the works. It is rigidly designed so nearly everything is formalized into phases - something very necessary in this type of game to minimize arguments over rules and effects - and it makes for a 'sensible' play experience. However, it takes awhile to play the game due to the slow slog of damage and counter healing/protection (this likely depends on the style and familiarity with the spells in the game). So not a game I am likely to own, but is a game I'm willing to play (if I have the time).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
When I first saw the components for this game, I didn't think I was going to enjoy it.  I'm not a fan of tactical games where players are moving units around the board and rolling dice for attacks.  So it was quite a surprise to me when I ended up really enjoying this game.  The game seems to have good strategic options and lots of replayability.  As far as dueling games go, I really enjoy this one.

Addressing the aforementioned concerns, the board only has 12 spaces, so while there is a bit of tactical maneuvering, this is not the main focus of the game.  And the dice add some randomness without being an overwhelming factor in the game.  This is because the range of numbers on the dice is only 0-2, rather than 1-6, and you're rolling lots of dice, which means over time luck with the dice should just about even out.

I was concerned that having one's entire deck at their disposal would create an analysis paralysis nightmare, but the fact that you can only pick two cards to use each turn actually limits the number of decisions you have to make and helps turns move more quickly than they would if you had access to your whole spell book throughout each turn.  And, obviously, having access to your whole spell book means you don't have to worry about not drawing the cards you need when you need them.  And the ability to customize the spellbooks with a sort of deck-building process really appeals to the CCGer in me.

UPDATE: After playing for the first time with constructed spell books, I still enjoy the game, but it is not a short game.  We played a 2v2 team game that took 2.5 hours, which longer than I like to put into a single game.  This has bumped my score from 8 to 7.  I still enjoyed it, but can't see playing it all that often.

+Nice balance of tactical play and deep strategy.
+Spellbook allows access to all of your cards every turn.
+Ability to customize your spellbook.
+Relatively simple rule set.
+Quality art and components.
+Lots of room for designers to explore in expansions.
+Once players are familiar with the game, it plays quickly.

-Having access to the entire spellbook can be intimidating for new players.
-Lots and lots of keywords require lots of flipping through the rulebook.  Really needed to include cheatsheets for these.
-A few fiddly rules and ambiguous card interactions (but what expandable card game doesn't have these?)

RATING: 9
This is by far one of my favorite games I have ever played. The mages are unique and well balanced, there are mismatches. 
It gives you the MTG or Yugioh fix that no other game does for me. It also eliminates all of the luck of the draw aspects that the other deck builders have.
Building the deck can take any where from a few min to a couple of hours depending on your experience.
The learning curve is quite steep and daunting to begin with.
There is enough in the box for 2 players without expansions but will leave you arguing on who gets what.

RATING: 8
Very good 2-player card game. Each player is a mage fighting his opponent in an arena. The game comes in a big box with space for cards and future expansions. It has a huge board and 2 spellbooks to place your cards in. Rules are not very complex but each card has a lot of abilities and keywords. The tutorial game takes about an hour but the full game took us over 3 hours! You have so many options in this game so it is prone to Analysis Paralysis. Nice artwork.

I admit that the game is very thematic and with a lot of tension and fun. I like the tactical options it provides. You just need a dedicated partner to get the most out of the game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
At first, Mage Wars seemed incredibly overwhelming and confusing to play. However, after our second play, my fiancée and I really enjoyed it and have found ourselves constantly wanting to play again. My advice would be to give it some time to get used to the rules and cards. We both are really enjoying it! 

It's also really cool because of the amount of customization that can be done without having to buy any cards or anything. We haven't begun to scratch the surface with that. I like the fact that the company has videos showing how gameplay works. I think with a relatively complex game, it's a really great idea. The codex is also really helpful. Overall, it's a really well done game. 

Also, the customer service with this game is great! I had a defective spell book, emailed customer service, and had a replacement in a few days!

RATING: 7
Azt hallottam, ez a játék a Summonerhez hasonló, csak annál jobb. Nos tényleg látszik a Summoneres ihletés, de ez más kategória: a Summoner szabálya kb. 5 perc, és az alap faj paklikkal azonnal nekiállhatunk a játéknak akár egy kezdővel is, egyedül a nyelvtudás a kritérium. A Mage Wars viszont szerintem kifejezetten gémer játék, gyakorlott kártyásoknak való, még erősebb a nyelvfüggőség is, és több a lehetőség. A Summoner is mélyíthető a kieg paklikkal, de a M.W. alapból is sokkal komolyabb. Nekem nem tetszett 1 parti alapján annyira, mint anno a Summoner az első játék után... De ez is egy olyan játék, amit több tucatnyi játék után kéne csak véleményezni.
Még egy megjegyzés a játék BGG pontszámához: anno amikor először megnéztem, amikor még csak pár százan szavaztak rá, akkor még szinte csak kamu szavazatokat adtak a játékra, az adta meg neki a kezdőlökést(értsd: több tucatnyi azonos időpontban bekönyvelt nick-ről egy-egy értékelés volt csak, a Mage Wars-ra adott 10-es)... Ezért fenntartásaim voltak a játékkal kapcsolatban, de végül is nem rossz.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is awesome, when you ever understand the rules. It takes a long time to get to know the game but if you know the rules, there is a whole new world opening. This game is hard to learn and hard to master. I always play it with the same person, and almost always lose, except if I search for strategies on the internet and make up a strategy in advance. Almost no change is in this game, only the attack and condition dice. This makes it super strategic and fun to play. As I said, I lose a lot with this game but after every game I am excited to start a new one with another (hopefully better) strategy and I always take a lot of time evaluating where it went wrong and what I could have done differently. Good game! Lost of fun and a lot of thinking. Because it takes a long time (including deckbuilding) I always am very into the game while playing and am almost doing roleplaying. I love this game!

RATING: 7.5
Take Magic the Gathering, add a board and don't restrict players' choices with a hand, but let them play whatever they wish from the entire grimoire. This is Mage Wars.
When I heard something like that, I thought it was insane and couldn't work.
But it works, and the game is great!
Mage Wars is the deckbuilder's dream, and it offers out of the box gazillions of options (something LCGs achieve only after MANY expansions and lots of money spent!).
And it is also an engaging game, one where you can't have a hand that plays itself, one with real interaction, and where skill matters.
A great, ambitious design. A great game.

RATING: 8.9
A very interesting take on a card game. Having a geographical element to consider adds another dimension to the traditional card game format. The deckbuilding experience without the death-by-bad-draw element is very attractive. This aspect really levels the playing field and provides an arena in which players can test and develop their strategic and tactical wherewithall. In the beginning the game can run a little long for a two-player affair. Have not yet tried the multi-player variant.

RATING: 9

RATING: 7
In the plethora of Magic the Gathering clones, Mage Wars really stands as the most innovative and compelling spinoff, way more enjoyable than its forefather: actually it takes the fun part of Magic (that is, casting spells and bashing your opponent) and combines it with a hint of theme (however thin it is), many dice to roll and a spellbook each player can choose his next spells from.

Not only makes all of this for a slightly more strategical game but it also brings lots of fun and interaction into the game, without having to waste a fortune on buying decks, boosters and single cards. [i](November 2013)[/i]

[b]Update[/b] - I’ve grown tired of Mage Wars quickly: just like Magic the Gathering does, a huge chore of this game too is building your own spellbook, which I can’t stand.

Since there’s no doubt a well-made spellbook benefiting from cards synergies has countless more chances to defeat a sloppier spellbook, most of my games are one-way only: this is why during my Fall 2015 rating revise I felt compelled to lower my rating from 9 to 7, the same as Magic the Gathering’s.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game is actually pretty sweet, however some of the downfalls have forced me to bring down my rating.  The main problem with the game is that it has so many keywords, and if you do not play regularly, each game can take a ton of time since you are constantly trying to remember how different things work. The 2nd big downfall for me is that it feels like the game can take too long to ramp up.  Everyone usually spends the first few turns just building their stuff up, which kind of kills the excitement for the first 30min or so.  Lastly, I'm sure if you play regularly, the deck building aspect is cool, however I just have zero interest in the deck building aspect.  I would have preferred to have preset mages, with a few customization options that came pre-balanced.

RATING: 9.9
Huge amount of rules to ingest but as with most games, just start playing and the penny eventually drops.

I've not played the full game yet - just the 'apprentice mode' - but it's fair to say that I've fallen head over heels in love with Mage Wars.

Fairly new (and late) to the world of gaming and still very much finding my feet, but I'm slowly discovering that I appear to be a 'theme junkie' and this game is dripping with it.

Arcane Wonders have succesfully managed to marry that great theme with what seems to be very high level tactical play, (chunky) dice chucking, and beautiful components. The result is nothing short of a masterpiece.

I can't commit to a 10 just yet, as I may just be experiencing the honeymoon period with this game. However I suspect it will remain one of my favourite games for a very, very long time.

However, have to look at the changes to Wizard's ability and Wizard's Tower if playing competitively.  But most probably I would not bother as I am playing this game casually.


Sortilege Wizard (Watergate Wizard) Water Wizard
Creatures: 3x Gremlin, 3x Hydra, 1 Huginn, 1x Gargoyle
Incantations: 1x Purify, 1x Minor Heal, 4x Teleport, 2x Force Push, 6x Dispel, 2x Seek Dispel, 6x Dissolve, 1x Shift Enchantment
Attack: 1x Fireball, 1x Geyser, 1x Surging Wave, 1x Jet Stream
Enchantments: 4x Nullify, 2x Jinx, 1x Regrowth, 1x Bull Endurance, 2x Rhino Hide, 2x Transfusion, 1x Harmonize
Conjurations: 1x Gate to Voltari, 1x Fog Bank, 1x Hand of Bimshalla, 2x Wizards Tower, 2x Mana Crystal, 1x Renewing Spring

Consider removing 2x Jinx

Get Gate to Voltari out at the start of your turn.
Summon Gremlins and Hydras supported by teleport
Hydras weakneses are Agony, Divine Protection, and Weak  There are however, 6 Dispels, a Dispel Wand and Purify.
Leverage on Voltaric Shield to provide early armour.
Cast Regrowth to withstand the onslaught.
Use zap and then guard to protect Gremlins.
Attack with Wizard Tower.
Cast two Mana Crystal.
Play Gate and Harmonize.
Teleport Opponent into Hydra.
Gorgon don't do much to Undead.
When opponent does not summon Mana channeling spells, then focus on getting armour out and regrowth.
Wiz Tower, Jet Stream and Surging Wave.
Damage Ramp.
Consider Corrode and Enchantment Transfusion

Red this for opening tactic - http://forum.arcanewonders.com/index.php?topic=13037.0

RATING: 9
Provisional rating (only played with 'apprentice' decks so far). OMG, I think this is the game I've been looking for; it's MtG mixed with WizWar. 

If you've ever played Magic the Gathering and been frustrated with mana screw or clump, or just wished there was a board where you summoned the creatures and moved around ... then give Mage Wars a try. You build your spell book (rather than a deck) and have all your spells available all the time. How cool is that? :)

And you put the cards on a board and move them around, taking turns to attack.

Yes, it's complicated, yes there are a bizillion keywords, all different and with no 'reminder' text next to them (wish they'd done that, like Magic does), but it oozes theme and now I've played I can't stop thinking about it, what sort of deck would I build etc.

Some will moan the dice destroy your plans, but I'm fine with a bit of random outcome in combat, this is a game of a mage battle after all, not a game of chess. When I want perfectly planned magic battles I'll play Mage Knight.

Not sure I'd ever play more than 2 player, but I feel the same about MtG.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
My first game was surprisingly fast even though I had never tried it before. Well, “fast” as in 1½ hours, but I had feared it would take 3-4 hours the first time (which some have said). Sure, I did miss what some of the cards did because of this, and perhaps I should have read the rules first and all of the cards thoroughly, but it was still a very enjoyable game in 90 minutes! 

I was playing against a somewhat experienced player with ~5 games under his belt, but I think I did quite good and wasn’t far from winning. I like that the game definitely seems to reward repeated plays (as you need to understand your own and your opponent’s decks and the combos you can make), but my biggest fear is probably that I, personally, don’t think I am going to play it enough to be truly competitive. The reason is that I have so many other “2 player only” games which I also need/want to play; mainly Commands & Colors: Ancients and Summoner Wars, which both fill the same niche and both play much faster. Oh, and I know that my wife will never want to play Mage Wars, so my main competitor will be my brother who suffers from AP in Summoner Wars – and I know he is going to make much slower decisions in Mage Wars when he has like 80 cards available every turn! So my personal gaming situation wouldn’t allow me to play this game much, so even though I liked it quite a bit the first time and would gladly play it again, I don’t need to own it.

I would imagine that it would be cool to build your own deck, but I simply don’t have the time for this so I’ll just play with the suggested pre-made decks. I liked the various types of cards: equipment, spells, creatures, enchantments etc.! I love that they play out very differently, and the bluff and tension involved about some of the enchantments/traps you put under your mage is awesome. There is less luck involved than in Summoner Wars because you choose which cards to play each round (compared to drawing blindly), but of course, there is still the bluffing (which some may think can be “lucky”) and a lot of rolling dice which can ruin even your best plans! Still, the better player who knows his and his opponent’s decks will win most of the time.

RATING: 3.5
A game where the designers said, "We don't like the randomness of card draw. Lets make a game where you have access to every card in your deck and you can just choose the cards you want. but then lets add in tons of luck of the dice."

Not a fan at all. Would rather take a deck of cards over a fistful of dice. Also there is no build up. The amount of mana you generate at the start of the game is the same as the amount you generate at the end. There are some cards that will boost the amount you generate so you might go from getting 10 mana a turn to 12. This makes it so you are always starved for mana and you don't feel this climatic build up where you get stronger over time. This is necessarily a bad thing but I didn't care for it at all.

It is also an extremely long game.

RATING: 10
A way to play a ccg within the culture of the people I game with these days. I buy the majority of the games and none of my friends are going to go heavy into a tcg/ccg/lcg. But with MWA we've invested in a couple base sets and all the expansions(or most so far, have to get Domination). We have all the cards in binders which we take turns having. 

MWA also has all the super fiddly rules HEAVY thing that I remember from my L5R days, but I've come to realize that part of me missed that fiddliness. We've had some loud disagreements about rules interpretations. This game seems to bring out strong emotions like some games do.

I started with the Force mage(who seemed an extra high level of difficulty herself) but am now rockin a druid deck. Playing Thursday, already excited. There is a masochistic element to Mage Wars in that I know all my carefully laid plans with be torn asunder but the journey is fantastic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Mage Wars is the better Magic the Gathering, because in MW you can cast any spell in your spellbook when YOU want (if you have the mana). You need not the luck to draw a specific spell as you have always access to your complete spellbook (you only have to choose which 2 spells you want to play this turn).
As the different magic schools are only important during the construction of your spellbook you don't have to waste time during the game -> you can concentrate in winning the game. 
I like the different mages (mostly Forcemaster and Priest) and I also like the idea of maneuvering the card-units in the 3 x 4 arena.
Neither in the Core Set nor in the first expansion (Forcemaster vs. Warlord) are broken cards and almost no cards have erratas until now. The designers of MW have made a very good job.
The only point I don't like is, that some powerful cards are only included 1x in the core set. As those cards are also only 1x in the two Tome-expansions I see no reason to buy those Tome-expansions. If you [like me] buy a second Core Set you also get a second dice-set so you don't have to share the dice during play.

RATING: 2
This game has so much potential.  Turning the traditional CCG deck into a spellbook was an excellent design choice that mostly works out very well to reduce luck.  However, the designers made the critical mistake of adding DICE to the game.  Why would you reduce luck in card draw just to add it back in (in much worse form) with dice?  It's also not as if the dice just get used occasionally.  They are used ALL THE TIME.  Could've been a great game, oh well.

RATING: 6
Update after first full play (using the other two mages we didn't use in the apprentice game): 
How in the world do I rate this thing? It's the only game where it took like 30 minutes to setup (build the decks), and that was even with following the instructions for the pre-build decks rather than building our own. And then the game took like 5 hours to play, maybe 4 with some downtime removed. But it is a [b]very[/b] rich experience. But I'm still not sure about the idea of choosing from a huge deck of cards every turn. Sometimes my brain just shuts down and refuses to process the choices. And it means for replayability I really [i]have[/i] to get into deck construction, to avoid the same openings every time. I'm not sure I can do that. I'll get at least a few more plays in with the prescribed decks.
---
Preliminary rating after first partial play. A hit with the son, but I fear it's too long for frequent play (we don't have huge blocks of time to dedicate). Chances are high I'm screwing up the rules. There are many possible things to get wrong. I don't quite have the determination to wade through pages and pages of rules questions.

RATING: 9.8
¡Qué pasada! Tengo que jugar más veces (aunque cuesta encontrar tiempo, las partidas son eternas), pero creo que ya puedo decir que es de los mejores juegos de duelo de magos que he jugado nunca. El factor azar es muy grande por los dados, pero un combate sin un poco de azar se hace muy puzzle. Un juego de combate sin dados pierde parte de gracia, y la estrategia a la hora de crear el mazo y elegir los hechizos en el turno, compensan la aletoriedad a la que te someten los dados. Aunque eso no quita que haya turnos en los que los maldigas, como hijos de satanás que son.
La variedad de cartas disponibles para la creación de mazo le da una rejugabilidad enorme, y el hecho de que cada mago tenga una manera completamente diferente de ser usado le da mucha profundidad. Algo sacacuartos, por la facilidad que tiene de ser expandido (que ya se está haciendo), pero por el lado bueno, se añaden más magos y con eso, más maneras de jugar. Muy recomendable a los fans de las batallas mágicas, y de magos en general. Te sientes realmente como un mago en mitad de una pelea, decidiendo cual es el mejor hechizo para la ocasión. Recomendable jugar con reloj de arena, la presión del tiempo límite le da un puntito muy interesante.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is an excellent game that truly captures the feeling of a one-on-one mage duel. The fact that you have access to your entire spell book at once as opposed to needing to draw your cards and hope you get something useful creates a lot of strategic depth. 

I have only a couple of issues with this game that keep it from a perfect score: 

-Setup and and teardown can be quite involved, and coupled with a long playtime means this one can be difficult to get to the table unless we carve out at least a few hours for it.

-The outcomes of dice rolls can be brutal; a bit less variance here would be welcome as sometimes very powerful cards can be wasted due to poor rolls on the dice, with not a lot of options to mitigate poor rolls. 

Despite these two complaints, I absolutely love this game.

RATING: 6
I really enjoyed this game. It "repairs" one of the major flaws in Magic (the bad draw) by letting you select your cards yourself.

Unfortunately, while it does have an effective remedy for this random chance aspect, it also introduces dice. To me, the dice ruin everything and turn what could have been a very strategic card-based boardgame into a game of luck.

I wish they had at least a deterministic alternative version or something.

RATING: 10
The black iron gate screeches as the old rusting metal lifts to reveal what awaits the arena... You emerge from the darkness - disciplined, focused and across the distance, beyond the bones and blood of the weak, you can see your opponent. As your eyes meet it becomes clear that only one will leave this arena. (spells at ready) Now... you begin your campaign to initiate your foe with the fallen. But are you man enough? - - are you MAGE enough?

Awesome Game! Great value in the box - plenty of cards, beautiful artwork, quality components and deep card customization options. Full disclosure/ This game has a moderate learning curve and can play out well beyond the suggested play time, BUT it is very much worth the time investment.

Gameplay is a blast - fun, challenging, balanced and it has a perfect splash of dice randomness. 

Support, support is the order winner - the Mage Wars forums are active and extremely helpful. Play-testers and the game creator engage with the community regularly to answer questions and tease what's to come. The first expansion is out (it's not essential, but FANTASTIC) and brings the total Mage count to six. I could easily see that number doubling between now and the end of 2014.

Fans of MtG, Summoner Wars, Wiz-Wars, and dare I say it... Chess (to an extent) - I highly recommend you give this one a shot. Shout out to Arcane Wonders/ job well done, you've got a masterpiece!

RATING: 9
*very* solid game, almost flawless. Magic the Gathering, done right, 20 years after its inception. Seriously: This very much feels like Magic the Gathering, sans Luck of the Draw (something that keeps nagging me in the old Garfield designs: mana/cash shortage or overabundance (unfortunately that even applies to Android Netrunner)), but with randomized combat and positional play. 

Downside is: too many keywords to start a quick game with anyone but a serious gamer. At least, the keywords are quite memorable and many things work the way you expect them to. 

Still, altogether a superb design.

UPDATE - Several more games played now and still enjoying this and very impressed. After initially playing as the Necromancer I have just played a first game as the Forcemaster - a completely different animal and game feel. 

After just a couple of plays I am very impressed. A game dripping in theme with lots of difficult decisions to make every turn.

RATING: 9
Fantastic game, rating likely to increase.

Think Magic the Gathering for real where the wizards and the monsters move around and attack each other. This coupled with the non-randomness (your whole spellbook is available to you from start) adds a whole new dimension to the game.

The problem is of course that with new players it can totally bog down. Luckily the creators have introduced some apprentice level starter spellbooks to mitigate this. Play with them a couple of times first to get the hang of the game before introducing the full spellbooks.

I haven't even started creating my own spellbooks yet, but someday I will and then the world of Etheria will tremble in fear. FEAR I SAY!

(or more likely fall over laughing)

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players are opposing Mages, duelling in an arena.  Large, lavish game where mages alternate taking actions.  Actions can be "full" - slow, powerful actions, or "fast" - quick actions which also allow movement before the action.  The battle takes place on a huge 4x3 square area.  Actions typically involve casting spells, prepared from a "spell book" (basically a CCG card display folder).  Spells come in six or seven categories - attacks, conjugations, creatures, enchantments, and so on.  Mages cast their spells, build up, summon help, and basically go to battle.  Combat is a clean, dice based vs armour affair.  I was quite impressed by this one.  Obviously a lot of thought and development has gone into it, and care taken to get it "right".  It plays very well, looks good, and is well supported by an excellent set of rules.  My only concern is the playing time, which may blow out depending on play style.  Still, easy to record and pack away, and set up again at a later date.  Impressive.

RATING: 10
Epic battle between two mages and their summoned familiars, minions, and conjured spells.  Mages can create monsters, summon equipment, shoot lightning bolts at each other, reflect said lightning bolts, cast horrible curses, and much more.  Plays like a grid-based miniatures game, but with cards instead of miniatures.  Unlike similarly-themed games, you do not have a hand of cards;  instead your entire spellbook is available to you the entire game, and you may cast any spell at your disposal - once.

RATING: 6.5
Mage Wars has a fan base for a reason. The developers have awesome ideas, and they're not afraid to develop them.

Not only this, but the developers have among the best fan service in the industry. Nowhere else are your suggestions and feedback as a consumer as likely to end up being incorporated into a build of the game.

So what is the game? Think of Harry Potter 5, where Voldemort and Dumbledore squared off. There was a sequence of spells like:
-Voldemort creates a snake made of FIRE
-Dumbledore extinguishes said snake by encasing Voldemort in a bubble of water
-Voldemort uses a shockwave to blast open the bubble and smash glass from the windows, and then send the broken glass towards Dumbledore
-Dumbledore turns the glass to sand

Throw in a couple of more creature summoning spells, and that's vaguely what playing Mage Wars feels like. It's probably closest to a miniatures war game, but using cards instead of plastic.

The unique mechanic is that you don't randomly draw cards into your hand. You select two cards per turn from your deck. If you don't use these - they go back into your deck. Suddenly, there's a whole new mechanic of ACTUAL TACTICAL THINKING that goes into hand management, rather than just "didn't get the card I wanted, better draw some more." Further, any killer combos you put in your deck - use 'em! But, your opponent will do the same.

This flexibility comes at a cost: game length. Expect 2 hours on a good day, and that's assuming you've already built your decks.

All said - an excellent game.

Edit 16/12/2015
Haven't played in yonks, considering selling.
Too much time required to play. Too difficult to teach.
Shame for all of the above reasons. Expansions look awesome as well.
Downrated to 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A lot of depth here and quite interesting for anyone interested in this genre as there will be plenty of new character expansions. You're a mage (d'uh) with your own spell book that you flip through each turn and pick two spells to play. There are quick spells and long-casting spells and you can do one of each per turn though you have to sacrifice moving to do a long-casting one. The spells are of all different types: defensive, offensive, creatures, enchantments, conjurations, etc. They provide an incredible amount of asymmetry and variability in what you can do. Each character is different (wizard, beast master, etc.) and has a different spell book. And flipping through the spell book to choose what to play gives a great sense of control which was refreshingly different than the usual card game fare. 

The game does seem like a commitment to a system rather than just a buy once and play type of game. You should probably go in expecting to buy further characters and spell books, but assuming you like the game, that's a feature not a flaw. You'll need to find a partner willing to invest in the time to become familiar with the game or else you'll crush everyone and nobody will want to play with you.

I'm not into games like this at all (2P attacky card games with a ton of cards) but for anyone who likes this genre, the design is tight (they spent 5 years playtesting) and it's a must try.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6

It's main appeal is the low cost of entry compared to actual miniatures, but I can see some players or the company itself gradually introducing minis eventually.

It takes up a lot of real estate on your table.

RATING: 9.5
Card driven grid combat game. A cross between summoner wars and magic the gathering.

Pros
-Unique (and revolutionary IMO) card game where all the cards in your deck are available to you at once
-Very solid game play, and well thought out use of dice combat (armor blocks attack, but critical rolls ignore armor)
-Unique character classes that have very different abilities and play very differently
-Customizable deck (with thematic point system) appeals to deck builders and TCG players (but pre-designed decks also made available)
-Lots of variety and lots of cards included in game

Cons
-Long game length (2+ hours)
-Can be complicated to learn/teach. Lots of reading as there are many cards in the game
-Lots of use of keywords. A few use of icons that newer players will struggle with initially
-Very detailed game flow (combat alone has something like 7 steps). Need someone familiar with the rules or you must have the rulebook must be kept handy

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
My favorite game - endless replayability and variety with the choices you have in mages, from those in the base game (Warlock, Wizard, Beastmaster and Priestess) to various expansions (Forcemaster, Warlord, Paladin, Siren, Druid, Necromancer). 

Game Length -- Some find the game too complex - too many keywords/traits, spell choices, strategies - but that's what I enjoy the most. Any question on keywords/traits are easily answered by a quick look at the codex. Plays much faster with two people familiar with the game. First few games may take 3 to 4 hours, but games between experienced mages rarely take longer than 60-75 minutes.

Option for 30 min games -- Highly recommend Mage Wars: Academy version if length/complexity puts you off. It's really like a Mage Wars: Arena-lite version, since it removes the arena (game board) miniatures aspect, which reduces complexity. Mages in Academy also have less life and overall spellbooks shrink from 60-75 cards (Arena) to 25-30 cards (Academy). Average playtime is only about 25-35 minutes!

Luck/Skill Ratio -- There are dice involved in combat but variance is lowered as results are only 0s, 1s, and 2s, but I'd put the luck/skill ratio of the game at about 5%/95%. If you're just getting it for yourself, may be a challenge to get people to learn game with you. If you have 4 people in your playgroup who play, Mage Wars: Arena is an incredibly rewarding, strategic game.

RATING: 5
Sort of Magic: The Gathering turned into an LCG, but different. There are lots of things to like in Mage Wars. Freely choosing your spells from your deck is a neat idea (although it can surely bog the game down as long as you're not familiar with the cards). The rules are somewhat lengthy and fiddly, yet if you've played other games of this type, they really are quite intuitive and once you've internalized the most important special abilities don't require looking up all that often. Sure, there still is a noticeable learning curve, but they did a really good job in that department.

Where Mage Wars doesn't convince me is the actual arena battle. It takes quite a bit longer than, say, a duel of Magic, yet doesn't offer a more interesting play experience. On the contrary, it looks to me like the part they concentrated on is the slow war of attrition which is my least favourite part of Magic. Ever so slowly grinding your opponent down just isn't very exciting, and a duel in Mage Wars is bound to go that way. If the battles were shorter and more to the point I'd be more interested.

RATING: 7
I played this game a lot when it first came out. I loved that you can prepare your deck of cards and that you have all the cards available to play them at any time. So, no luck of the draw! Cool! There is also a spatial element that was quite new for a card game at the time. The dice rolls are lots of fun (you can even roll 10 dice at a time). I was so into it that I became play tester for the expansions. And a little after that (after the first expansion I worked on) the game started losing its appeal. Instead of being more exciting it became a chore. Play testing is hard. Lots of note taking, session reports, tweeks, card printing and print and playing... I play tested for a couple of expansions and then I quit and never played again. My son still looooves it and continues to play to this day but I unfortunately turned the page. 

It's cool though that my name appears in a couple of rule books and I appreciate the fact that Arcane Wonders approached me and were super professional to work with and very caring about the fans and the franchise. The team at Arcane Wonders rocks! Seriously. 

P.S.: Bryan Pope is the coolest guy in the industry.

RATING: 8
I never played MtG, but if this is what it was trying to do, I can see why people got addicted.

It's possibly the most thematically driven game in my collection (with the exception of Earth Reborn and Shadows of Malice). The tactical decisions I make based on board positioning and the spellbook feel like they carry a logic that isn't dictated so much by the rules as it is by common sense and some level of immersion in the conceits of the game. The rules are there as a jargon framework.

It's really satisfying to play and equally satisfying to compose a deck (spellbook).

The only drawback: it requires two dedicated players of comparable skill.

RATING: 9
Shockingly good card/board game hybrid that turns the rules of a card game on its head. Practically every card game ever invented has one thing always in common - random card draw. Here however you're constructed deck is not in a shuffled pile but as a spellbook, which you can flick through and pick cards at will.

This makes the magic in this game FEEL like magic should. You're not sitting there hoping to draw the creature that will win you the game but instead just using that creature exactly when you want to. Anything that can happen will happen and if something has a counter then you can always use that counter. 

Seriously; get this game

RATING: 10
I bought Mage Wars a few months ago and have been playing a few times a week since then. I immediately fell in love with the game and its, so far as I know, unique "spellbook" mechanic. There's lots to love about Mage Wars. It has a deceptively simple set of moving parts that ramp up as the game progresses. The differences between the four types of mages that come in the core set are interesting and present a surprisingly diverse array of strategies. When you combine the different play styles of each mage with the customization options of building your own spellbook for each it all adds up to a game with satisfying variety.

Finally, I have to comment on the real superstar feature of this game that will possibly go unnoticed by some, but will be readily apparent with more experienced gamers. Mage Wars has superb game balance. All of the various spells and abilities "feel" very balanced. When I win it always feels like I have to really try to out-think my opponent, and when I lose I can usually trace it back to a bad decision I made rather than the power of any one spell or ability.

This is not, however, a game for players who suffer from analysis paralysis. The heart of the game is in the planning phase where each player takes turn selecting spells from his or her book. If there is one suggestion I may make it is that players agree to a reasonable time limit for the planning phase. I've found that 2 or 3 minutes ought to get it done. Of course, I would not impose this restriction on a learning game. 

With that said, I have not had much of a problem teaching this game. If you allow the more experienced player the opportunity to go first, and if he or she explains each of the actions he or she is taking clearly, then it should only take 2 or 3 "rounds" before a new player gains a good understanding of what is going on. 

So, if you love wizards and strategy battles then I hope you will give this game a chance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Magic:the Gathering meets Summoner Wars and is better than either of these. You are a magic user in a contest of cunning and power against the opposing mage. Each turn you will select spells to play from your entire "deck" rather than randomly drawing from a deck and put them into play representing creatures to defend and attack, spells to heal, increase factors or inflict direct damage. This is M:tG with a spacial element or Summoner wars with lots of magic and combos.

The basic rules are quite straight forward and although the added detail is considerable - the rules are very intuitive - top marks! The rule book is very well written and there is a good index of all the spell key-words which constutute the bulk of the game's detail. Icongraphy on the cards is excellent and will become easy to recognise after a few plays.

Components are all excellent overall although I would recommnd sleeving the cards which may be a little thin. The card art is very good.

This game will require an investment of time and interest to play well but if you want an epic game of battles between mages including all of the spell casting, setting up combos, and spacial considerations of a skirmish wargame, plus the meta game of deck building then Mage Wars may just be the game for you. Excellent.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I love this game. Unabashedly. It's shameful how much I geek-crush on Mage Wars.

I want to play it every time I see it in my library, but it sits there, calling out to me, spell books sitting in a pile on the shelf, and nothing happens. 
Mainly because my son doesn't like it.
In fact, he hates it. 
My daughter isn't into the whole game-geek stuff, so she's a no-go, and the wife?
Forget about it.

You should get a shrink-wrapped person included with this game so you have someone to play it with. This is as nerdy as it gets, and you will have a hard time getting someone to sling spells with you for the several hours it takes to get this game going.

What it offers, however, is sublimely brilliant.

It gives you the opportunity to strategically build a spell book that you will use in the game. You can create this book an hour before you play, or you can create it while you shave the morning before. Or while you're on the bus, or eating diner, or doing any mundane task, because your mind will be spinning away thinking of how you can best combo a fireball spell with acid, so you melt away your opponents armor before you cook him like a brisket.

Creating the spell book is an "out-of-game" activity that will occupy several hours of your life, and you'll love every minute of it. SO. MANY. OPTIONS.
THEN you get to take that book, crackling with blue-tinged potential, into the arena and try it out against a living, breathing, bastard. 
That's right, a bastard. 
Because they will have done the same thing with their spell book, creating a Necromancer who swarms you with poison spitting zombies before you can ever get in position to launch your wall of fire. Or they may have built a "Star Wars" worthy Force-Master, who flings giant boulders at your head, or just backs you into a corner and devastates you with a conjured mallet the size of a Buick.

It's so cool. 

But it's hard. You actually get to feel like a real mage with a book of spells, pulling out every trick just to survive, and survive you must, because this is a last-person standing game. And no one likes to loose.
 
Aside from the difficulty, and the time investment, the only other complaint I have is the status markers. There are A LOT of status markers. Some make creatures more powerful, or cause effects like traps and tripping vines. The board, as large as it is, gets cluttered with cards, and status tokens, and hidden tokens, and blood counters, and damage markers- It can get a little overwhelming. You can also end up in analysis paralysis picking the two cards you're going to use this round, while trying to predict what you opponent will do. 
But that's the beauty of it. You don't know what they will do. They may have brought forbidden magic from other schools, so that cute Beastmaster may suddenly summon an ancient demon to kick your ass, or your ecco-friendly Druid might suddenly teleport right in front of you and beat you to death with her wand. It's all possible, and infinitely satisfying.
Except for that whole "Need another person to play," thing.
That part sucks.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[Mage Wars](http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/101721/mage-wars): Es un juego para dos jugadores aunque se puede jugar a 4 por equipos. Lo hemos porbado Awell y yo recientemente y hemos realizado 2 partidas por lo que estos comentarios entran en la parte de primeras impresiones.  

**Componentes**  
- Bastante impresionantes, la caja es enorme y trae huecos para futuras expansiones y para poder añadir mas componentes, el tablero y los componentes se ven bastante binen, incluida la calidad de las cartas que trae que no están nada mal teniendo en cuenta que es producción China.

![](http://farm8.staticflickr.com/7400/9559821479_48ae6e3c37.jpg)

**Las Reglas**  
- Las reglas están muy bien estructuradas y son sencillas de entender, de seguir y de consultar, trae incluso unos apéndices y glosarios para poder consultar, todo esto se agradece porque la primera partida suele ser bastante tortuosa.

![](http://farm4.staticflickr.com/3830/9562612888_73808f767b.jpg)

**Al Turrón**  
- El tema del juego es simple, somos dos magos que nos damos de hostias, cada uno de una escuela de magia diferente, Cada mago tiene un libro de hechizos que viene a ser un álbum para meter las cartas y es que todo el juego son cartas. Se preseleccionan los hechizos o te construyes el tuyo propio gracias a unas reglas bastante simple y eliges entre las 300 y pico cartas de hechizos que vienen en el juego básico, obviamente la elección del mago marca la escuela de hechizos que vas a seguir principalmente. Obviamente nosotros jugamos con mazos preseleccionados.

- La primera partida fue en modo aprendiz, te dan unos cuantos hechizos, no muchos para que no te hagas la picha un lio y tienes un mago que mas que mago es un aprendiz, no tienes habilidades especiales ni características únicas, se juega en la mitad del tablero y ale. Esta primera partida suele ser algo tediosa y al final uno espera matar al otro mago esperando a que se muera de muerte natural, miras las cartas con cara de estúpido y no sabes que es una conjuración o para que leches puede servir tal o cual carta. Al final, la partida cumple su objetivo, conocer un poco el juego y el personaje que estás usando.  

![](http://farm6.staticflickr.com/5530/9559823661_78acfafb7a.jpg)

- La segunda la jugamos completa con los mazos preseleccionados para los dos personajes que elegimos: Hombre de las bestias Perro Flauta y Sacerdotisa de Berska. La partida fue mucho mas fluida y mas a saco, ya sabiamos lo que hay que hacer y perder el tiempo en este juego es darle oportunidades al contrario para que se fortalezca, el libro de hechizos te permite seleccionar un montón de posibilidades en cada turno desde marcarte una estrategia a intentar reaccionar a un ataque enemigo, cada turno se seleccionan 2 hechizos y se ponen boca abajo, estos son los dos hechizos que vamos a poder jugar por turno, cada criatura incluido nuestro mago tiene la posibilidad de hacer una acción por turno, esta acción puede ser mover y hacer una acción ligera o hacer una acción completa, asi que nuestro mago va a ir invocando criaturas para combatir contra el enemigo y a la vez va a utilizar encantamientos, ataques mágicos, conjuraciones o hechizos para obtener ventaja, el juego es muy narrativo y la verdad es que nos lo pasamos francamente bien.

**Conclusiones de estas primeras impresiones**  
- El juego viene a ser como jugar al Magic en tablero o es lo que tenía que haber sido Sentinels of the Multiverse, en este tienes mucha sensación narrativa y eres un mago que está lanzando hechizos para cepillarse al otro. Es un juego que pide jugar y desarrollar y el libro de hechizos es un puntazo, cada turno te toca ojear y seleccionar los hechizos que vas a lanzar, es muy evocativo. si quereis un juego fantástico y estais dispuestos a invetir el tiempo necesario en aprender y jugarlo porque me da que algunas partidas pueden alargarse al infinito echarle un ojo.

RATING: 6
Mage Wars strong similarities here to Summoner Wars, but the latter fits my style better. Mage Wars is very strongly focused on being a "tournament game". It really encourages you to customize your deck and try to beat your opponents who also own the game. Something like Summoner Wars does a little better job of being pick-up-and-play right out of the box, I think. For example, in Mage Wars, you have a deck of about 25 cards or so, ALL of which you can choose to play at the beginning of the game. (You have a "spell book" instead of a draw pile.) This makes it pretty daunting, but it does seem to reward this obsessive play with good strategic depth in decisions.

The illustrations and components are good quality and the variety and quantity in expansions and supplements is strong. The various decks play rather differently from each other, and provide interesting match-ups. 

At this stage in my life, I play games more casually than competitively and the 2-player battle game genre is a crowded field. Because of that, this game is really not one that I will probably ever purchase, yet I'd be happy to play a few games with someone who doesn't mind a severely outmatched opponent. I think this game will probably always be a niche player, with a core group of rabid fans but largely forgotten or unnoticed by the general gaming public. This kind of game may appeal to you, but be aware you will likely need to find opponents who own and play as much as you do to find it remotely challenging.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This is a tough one for me to rate.  After abandoning CCGs in the late 90s partly because of the collectable nature and partly because of the time I spent building decks, I decided that I would not ever return to that type of game, even though they provided rich and enjoyable games.  LCGs came along and promised to remove some of the annoyance, but I held fast.  Summoner Wars introduced the idea of a spacial element to something similar to a LCG, but it did not grab me - even though I specifically liked the lack of an intensive deck building element.  Mage Wars, however, sucked me in after one play with a (very enthusiastic) friend.

I have subsequently wasted much more time that I would care to admit agonising over deck choices.  That said, the limited points system when building decks causes you to focus your strategy which perhaps speeds up the process.  Each mage's unique combination of abilities also help you to make decisions when selecting between cards.

I am generally a dice hater, the total opposite of my dice loving, role-playing former self.  However, Mage Wars adopts a system using D3s (six side dice labled 0, 1, 2 on two sides each) and requires pools of usually 3 to 6 dice to be rolled at a time, so it really minimises the variation from the mean during the course of a game.  Also, each die roll has a mean result of 1 (the sum of the numbers on each face divided by 6) so it is very easy to understand the likely strength of any roll.  So I love the dice system.

Finally, I tend to avoid card games which encapsulate the majority of the rules in the card text because of the time-consuming learning curve.  I tend to prefer board games with clean, predictable systems, with few exceptions, which build tension from players' choices alone.  Somehow, Mage Wars, with its exceptionally clean and intuitive system, and perhaps some small appeal to the fantasy nerd still somewhere inside me, has smashed through that prejudice too.

So, would I recommend it?  Absolutely!  However, I feel as though any rating sitting alongside other games in my collection is a little out of place given the stark contrast with the other games.  After around half a dozen plays I will award it a conservative 9.  We'll see how that lasts!  It may go up, but I fear that I am rating games too highly at the moment...

Update: this has to be a 9.5.  As much as I hate to admit it...  For me, this is the ultimate, streamlined conclusion to the story set in motion by Richard Garfield in the early 90s.  One thing which has become apparent is that it seems impossible to exploit the game systems to yield some sort of technical advantage.  For example, if you dispel your opponent's spell, it will cost you exactly the same amount as it cost them to cast it.  If anything appears to offer the possibility for you to profit in this sort of scenario then there is always an associated risk which has to be judged.  This leaves you, instead, to focus on combining the more qualitative strategies when building a deck - and should prevent expansions from causing "card inflation" in the future.  The attention to detail by the designer in this respect is exemplary.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
A great game and system, just bogged down with unnecessary fiddly-ness and longevity. It is a pretty involved two player game, with as many keywords and idiosyncrasies as Magic The Gathering. Not to mention a huge board to move, about 100 counters and 19 different status effects, just to name a few. A game will take 90-120 minutes, which is pretty long for a two-player game IMO. I loved building spellbooks and finding great strategies with each of the different mages, but it needed much more streamlining.

One thing I hate is some of the decisions Arcane Wonders did with the game itself. I hate small cards in games...but this game could've definitely benefited from it. When you play a monster, the card itself goes on the board. The bad thing is that multiple creatures can be on one tile and will, I repeat, WILL overflow with a wealth of information since there is 10 creatures on one given space. With smaller cards, spaces wouldn't feel so crammed with all the cards. Sadly this would need heavy streamlining to the games system, cause fitting all the texts each card has would be an eyesore to everyone. 

Academy, looks to have all the best parts of Arena, but eliminating all the unnecessary bits and getting straight to the action. Probably the biggest waste of money on my part. Wish I did more research. Live and learn I suppose

RATING: 3
Many players love this game, but it just doesn't get my dopamine flowing.  I respect that it is a well designed and well produced game that does what it sets out to do exceedingly well.  I prefer Euro games.  Mage Wars is a two player game in which the players assume the role of some fantasy hero locked in deadly combat with the other player's character.  The game consists of playing cards that summon creatures who battle on their master's behalf or casting magic spells that deal damage or heal or do one of a great variety of other effects.  I concede that players have a great number of interesting choices to make, both before the match in composing their spell book and in making strategic plans and tactical battle.  Even with all of the sundry cards that are played, there is much dice rolling / randomness in the combat.  LOL I just can't imagine that rolling a six sided die is representative of swinging a sword and attempting to harm an enemy--sorry ;p

[hr]
2012-10-24

Initial Rating: 8.5 (October 2012)

WOW I’m impressed. One of the best games I have played recently. I have only played it twice in the apprentice mode but I REALLY like what I see. I hope I can play this often if I can find some friends who want play this on a regular basis.

First of all the game appears to be complex but it is very easy to teach. I was able to teach the game under 16 minutes. It is true that the game is designed to flow logically as if magic was real. The components are top notch, the art is GREAT, one of the best I have seen. At first I didn’t want to play with the spell books, but they really add to the atmosphere AND make easy to quickly scan your spells.

The spells are very well done and it appears all the wizards are well balanced. I love the theme and flavor text on the cards.

The only randomness comes from the dice but as you can plan and react to it, it doesn’t really bothers me, it even excites me :D. The combat is simple but fun.

So far I have encountered lots and lots of hard (and great) tactical decisions. As you can only (generally) choose 2 spells each round the decision of what to play is hard. I have only played with pre constructed spell books so I have not yet grasped the strategic facet of the game but I will do it soon.  

I played years and years of MtG and both games have some similarities, but Mage Wars eliminated the random aspects that MtG had. I love it. It also has some similarities with Summoner Wars but the decisions are much more complex so I like Mage Wars a lot more. I would love to have some miniatures for the monsters but the cost would be very high so the cards are ok. I can’t wait to buy the expansions they already announced :).

My only concern so far is the play time. I have heard reports of games that took more than 5 hours. My plays were around the 70 minute mark (but where in apprentice mode). Also if both players are making their decks it can take a long time but I guess it will eventually decrease as the players begin to know the cards.

In conclusion, a game that has the potential to enter my Top Ten Favorite Games list, great gameplay (I didn’t feel any downtime), great art, great game. If you like Fantasy themes and/or Tactical fighting with miniatures give it a shot.

Current Rating: 8.5

2012-10-25

Can’t wait to play it with the full spell book. Perhaps today :D.

I always want to play this so I´m giving it a new rating. Mage Wars is now officially in my Top Ten Favorite Games of all time.

[hr]
2016-02-21

I still love Mage Wars. I find it to be an outstanding game and I always want to play it, however doing so has proven hard. The main reason is the steep learning curve with the spell books and the high playtime. I think I need to play with the tournament rules to avoid games that exceed the 3 hour mark. Mage Wars is certainly a lifestyle game, you need to spend a lot of time getting better and I haven’t found many friends willing to do this with me. I will still buy all things Mage Wars and I plan to make an effort to play it more often.

Arcane Wonders have an excellent customer service. I bought Mage Wars: Arena thinking it was a new game, but it was a new edition of the base game and they offered to refund me the money (which I refused). So now I have two base games and all the expansions and promos I could find. 

Having saying all that, I’m still downgrading its rating for the reasons stated. Mage Wars is a game I won’t ever sell, but my desire to play it has decreased a bit during the past year. I still have to try Battlegrounds and Academy.

RATING: 9
Very enjoyable game, very strategical but with just enough randomness that the players still have to deal with unexpected events without the game feeling chaotic.  None of the spells seem too overpowered and there doesn't appear to be any mechanics in the game that can be exploited too much.

However, despite this meticulous attention to balance, the game still allows for a very flexible playing style.  Players can choose and customise their spell books, meaning that they can refine their strategy between every game.  As a result, the game has a significant amount of longevity.

The only downside is that, to get the most out of the game, you need to find a playing partner who is willing to invest a reasonable amount of time in learning to play and building spell books.  The Mage Wars playing community is still pretty small where I live, and so I don't get to play it as often as I'd like.

RATING: 2
It needs some work on the mechanics...

edit: Played the final copy today... the enchantment mechanic does create some element of surprise.

The problem right now is that the game has a very high learning curve. Because you can play any card at any time, the first game is overwhelming since you have to read through so many cards. This is a very difficult game to just pick and play or introduce to others right away, because they have to read a ton of cards. In drawing card games, you only have to read your hand and go along. In this game, new players have to "prepare" by learning their deck first. My opponent (a Summoner Wars veteran) actually gave up after learning the game because he needed more time to "think about" the cards before playing.

Furthermore, there's just too many keywords to follow. You can have to constantly look in the rulebook to know what your cards do.

Finally, despite all the layers of superfluous complexity, the core of the game is really just dice rolling. There's a lot of abilities but they just seem to be generally good. Because there's no hand/deck management and mana management is simplified, I don't really see how you can develop interesting tricky combos like in Magic, which renders the whole point of having a spellbook ineffective. Plus if you could play a hypothetical killer combo every single game, wouldn't that reduce the re-playability of the game?

RATING: 6
I really liked the diversity among the characters and styles.  The idea of having all your spells available to choose from for each turn makes for a very different experience than other card games and the simple positioning/combat mechanics work well.  

Unfortunately, I've had to drop it several points because the balance among the mages appears to be way off.  Wizards seem to be the golden children, possessed of the best spell selection, basic character abilities and familiar (the wizard's tower) in the game.  They are so powerful that if you were serious about being competitive then I'm not sure why you would take any other class.  Another class *may* have the answers to a particular situation - a wizard *will*.

RATING: 8
A very well-designed if thinky dueling game with a metric ton of pregame customization options and the novelty of full control of one's spellbook from the first moment to the bitter or beneficent end. It also cleverly combines deck construction with a spatial/board element that increases the complexity and the richness. Expansions seem to build out the experience without significant additional rules overhead or power creep. The downsides? It's a bit--probably more than a bit--too heavy for casual play and benefits from one or more dedicated opponents. Maximal control means potentially extensive preparation as well as in-game paralysis as each move is evaluated. The cards and art are also well done, but aesthetically the game does nothing for me, really. I would definitely play this one more if I had more time to devote to it and I had people with whom to play more regularly. My wife actually quite likes this one, so maybe it will see more time soon.

RATING: 9
The game has a fairly steep learning curve, however, the game has rather intuitive mechanics and interactions. The game is a lot like a chess in the first few turns where each player takes their first few actions setting up their favored strategy and goal, but as the opponent builds their side you will need to adjust accordingly.
But this is where Mage Wars really shines, because while you react to your opponent, your opponent will be reacting to you. And then the concept of tempo and prediction become SO much more important.
I knew the game was a keeper when I decided to protect a vital piece of equipment the moment before an opponent tried to destroy it. Or when I tried to teleport out of a trap only to find my opponent had known I would do that and nullified my teleport.
So you will be building up a system to destroy your opponent while trying to hinder their system, but at the end of the day, the game is won by dice rolls. Which is unfortunate for a game that is so wanting to be heavy strategy, but those are some of the most fun moments when a final blow can turn into a flop or a desperate spell turns into a huge bang against the opponent.
Mage Wars does plenty of great things I have seen before. I have played many table top and card games and you can see a lot of what Mage Wars is doing takes inspiration for that. However, the game does tempo and prediction and anticipation better than pretty much any other single game I have played. This game makes every turn after the first few into a vital decision, and when you end up playing things exactly like you wanted or end up sitting on your spells the whole turn, you will remember and rethink each of those decisions the rest of the day.

RATING: 9
I will be honest—much of the fun in this game comes from the simple addition of an all-at-once kind of spellbook. Holding a physical thing and leafing through to select your spells is thrilling and scratches the itch that had been tickling the back of my mind since I bought my first Magic cards in 1997. Never before have I felt as much like a wizard.

Mechanically, too, this is an everything-AND-the-kitchen-sink kind of deal, with a few smart toilets (as an aside, those are actual things now) thrown in for good measure, but it feels remarkably coherent and surprisingly unbloated. It is also delightfully tactile and feels more kinetic than any turn-based board game has a right to be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Believe it or not...this HAS take over Summoner Wars...

The game is so deep...so there will be room for both, but the amount of options, variety in play styles and future promises are staggering.

everything one could want in an epic-feeling duel of mages.

*Absolute custom creation of your spellbook with no luck-of-the-draw.
*You control the mana element, so no mana srewage.
*You can react to what your opponent has done, is doing or is trying to do with your next move/prep phase
*You can hope for a huge roll of the dice to swing the battle back in your direction. And get that awesome feeling of 'Huzzah!'
*You can realistically build many diffeent types of decks whilst they not be limited to that style of deck. Example, you can make a largely "Mana Depletion" spellbook. But, if that strategy fails, or if the ebb and flow of the battle as it unfolds changes your thoughts...you have other things in your deck to get the job done. Some creatures, some attack spells, etc.

There are more points to make; these are but a few of the beautiful things about Mage Wars.



An incredibly simple concept (look through your deck (which is a spellbook) and choose what you want to do this turn) just works so well.

Another geek here wrote the following here: "I love this game - it has all of the best parts of other games - cards with out deck randomization - mana with no mana flood/screw - battle without measuring - creatures without painting - & lots of dice rolling"

This is a concise way to sum up my feelings.  It's just incredible.
It's a a 10. I can give that considering all aspects of the game and universe it's set in.
Gameplay may be a bit long, for some.  But I don't mind investing some time into a great match like this!  Set up, if you want to change Mages out of the spellbooks, or devise a new spellbook from scratch, also takes time, but for some, that adds to the enjoyment!

I am astounded by this companies efforts in the artistic category...for a 1st time product it is AWESOME.  Remember early Magic the Gathering artwork???  A bit on the weak side, for most cards...this one is consistently incredible.
It is quite quality.

Update 11/12.  The more I play, the more I like.  It's astounding how every game is much different from the last match played.  Even using the same spell decks...the reactionary system makes this possible.  Unbelievably good and I will be a customer/player for the loooong, foreseeable future!  Arcane Wonders, congrats and best of luck with this one for the long haul!!!

RATING: 8
Mage Wars is a singular experience.  The game represents two mages thrown into an arena with nothing but a spellbook.  Thematically, the game pulls you right into their shoes.

I love this game.  It will be in my top 10 for sure.  It has everything you need for a fabulous duel.  My only complaints are that it is a bit too complex for new players, and first-time matches take a bit too long for my liking.  This is a direct result of the fact that players have continuous access to their entire decks of cards, and new players tend to get bogged down in too many options.  This complaint goes away quickly once a player becomes familiar with the decks.  

The game allows almost limitless customization, giving players a chance to build truly unique decks.  The game comes with way more cards than a player will need to build a deck around almost any playstyle.

The game components are great, and the card artwork is top-notch.  I do have some complaints about the box insert -- it does not provide a functional way to store unused cards.  However, this is a more minor problem and should not detract from an otherwise great game.

RATING: 7
When Summoner Wars came out there were all these inexplicable (to me) comparisons to Magic: the Gathering that I didn't get. I guess summoning creatures was kind of like Magic, but nothing else about the game was even close. Mage Wars, however, does share a lot with MtG. It has the summoned creatures, but also enchantments, instants, equipment, mana sources, and other things. But it does two very different things. First, there is a grid where your creatures (and self) get all movey and fighty. Second, the spellbooks. MW is like a deck-construction game in which you have access to your whole deck at once, and that's just a great idea, even if it's a bit overwhelming at first. It makes a huge difference.

This sort of game is almost always not for me, but Mage Wars...something about it works for me. This is something I can handle and do okay at unlike, say, Summoner Wars or Dungeon Command, both of which were good games I have no interest in or aptitude for. What MW's difference is, I can't really say, but more plays may reveal it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've now got a good 10 games in and this game keeps on unlocking so much excellent game play it's amazing! I've played against 3 different opponents and I've got to say that if you've got an equal opponent then the decisions become very tense and very, very meta (which I LOVE!).

However, it's got to be said that there is a very big downside (actually there are 2): 1) first-time players are going to be struck down by AP. It's going to happen even if you streamline the experience. 50+ spells at your fingertips? Of course, AP is going to happen. 2) the first few games take quite a bit longer than games that happen after you've got a few under your belt (and I mean QUITE a bit longer). This can't really be helped but there it is.

I'm really looking forward to the upcoming expansions and while I'm not an expansion nut I have high hopes that these expansions will really continue to grow the game and the overall experience.

No other 'Mage/Wizard' duel game gives me this feeling of actually hurling spells at my opponent. MagicTG never even came close as I always felt like I was just dealing with numbers stacked on numbers. The only other 'card' game that appeals to me in that visceral way is Netrunner and I think that may come from the completely asymmetric game play. I think that this outblasts Summoner Wars as well simply because again, I don't feel like a mage tossing spells and incantations at my opponent, it stills feels like cards. Mage Wars feels real, for whatever that's worth... and for me, that's worth more than just about anything else in a game about conjuring creatures, flinging incantations and fireballs and spells of rot at my opponent.

Rating's going to go up but I really want to see what kind of longevity this has over say a few months of game play before I reassess.

UPDATE (Dec.2012) - I've now got over 50 games under my belt and this game is THE BOMB! Utterly engaging from start to finish, I've even got my wife to start playing and she's really enjoying the experience. This game has found not only a regular rotation amongst my 2-player games but has also become the de facto choice for games night as both me and another gaming friend have convinced the other members to purchase their own copies. We bring our ready-made spellbooks (2) to each games night and go about annihilating each other.

I'm looking forward to the expansion more than I can say for any other game that I own or have owned... ever. This is easily one of my top 5 favorite games. I can't wait until my 7 year-old son can engage in mage combat with his dad in the next few years.

RATING: 7
This one lies somewhere between D&D, Magic the Gathering, Dream Blade, and Summoner Wars.

While this system isn't totally no-luck (there's still a 33% chance per die to do no damage), taking the random card play out of the equation is something that's never been done before. 

The sky really is the limit for this system and I see no reason that this won't become a 10 for me.

Update:

Still a good game, but it never became that 10 due to lack of continued support from the publisher, play time, and learning curve which turned off opponents.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Gameplay:
+ extremely rich and very complex, but quite intuitive mechanics, which work perfectly well with the theme; every card does feel like a spell indeed
+ you have access to all cards in your spell book at all times, which reduces randomness and enhances tactical play
+ customizable deck building allows for infinite possible tactics and personal play style

+/- dice decide combat outcome; I don't mind it, in fact I rather like it, since it triggers some more emotions, but some people may not like randomness in this otherwise extremely tactical game

- some rules are very complex and can be overlooked quite easily
- a match can take quite a while, especially if someone turtles
- having access to all cards, can be overwhelming and it can definitely end in analysis paralysis.


Components:
+ high quality components
+ very gorgeous artwork on the cards
+ a good box insert allows for some sorting at least for 2 expansions.

side-note:
if you don't like this game only because it is too long, play Mage Wars Academy. almost the same, but far shorter.

RATING: 8
Mage Wars has quickly risen to be one of my favorite games. Will it stay there? Time will tell. 

The concept of having a spellbook with all the cards in your "deck" available to you is novel and incredibly well thought out. The card art is amazing. It's as good as MtG art. The huge variety of play with different mages, their different play styles, and different spellbooks is dizzying (in a good way). Throw on top of that the option to customize or build a new spellbook from scratch... wow!

The game presents the player with many interesting and purposeful tactical, strategic, and metagame decisions. I liked the game so much after my first session playing it that I immediately bought $100 in expansions.

UPDATE 05/24/15: It seems my love affair with Mage Wars is waning. I like it, but I find myself wanting to play other games more.

RATING: 8

RATING: 8
Mage Wars 8
Two games are hardly enough to scratch the surface of this one, even if we upgraded the apprentice rules a bit for the second game. What I can say so far is that the apprentice rules are an obvious blessing, allowing an immediate entry into the game with the first duel lasting about an hour instead of the 4+-hours reported by the unlucky folks who jumped in at the deep end.

And while the first game mostly consists of finding your way around the rules, the second one (with 3 spells added to the apprentice spell books and the stats and rules for the individual mages in play), the second one already offered enough variety to allow me to make strategical mistakes instead of rule blunderings. Progress! From what I can say, it’s going to be a steep climb until I can play this fluently, but so far it’s also an enjoyable one. My recommendation would be to concentrate on playing instead of winning; seeing how stuff actually works in play will allow your skill to grow faster than long ponderings over your spell selection every round.

I appreciate very much that the game designers obviously put a lot of thought into the balance of depth and accessability, and I’d say that the managed to hit the sweet spot, at least for me. Since I value content over packaging, I’ll readily forgive the few instances where their lack of experience shows – minor stuff like counters that could be a 6 or a 9, inconsistent use of symbols/text for attack types ... apart from these nits, the game is very polished and seems to have avoided the trap of underdevelopment that so many bright ideas from newcomers tend to fall into.

RATING: 2.5
TLDR: Really good, except everything boils down to dice, which completely ruins the entire thing. If you don't mind that, then highly recommendable.

I really wanted to like this. Played it 4 times in total, each game way too long. To me, the game disguises itself as a very strategy-heavy game. And at first glance, this also seems to be the case. I really like Magic, and the mechanics of Mage Wars really appealed to me. But despite the nice deck building, the nice abilities and mechanics (which are all very nice!), the game just boils down to... dice. You plan, you wait, your opponent walks right into your trap, ha ha, 8 dice in your face! ... 3 damage... And he throws 5 dice and deals 4 damage. *slowclap* So much for so little. So disappointing. If the game didn't have dice, this would be an amazing game. Love everything else about the game. But this one thing just ruins the entire thing.

RATING: 9
The easiest way to describe Mage Wars is a combination of Magic the Gathering meets a tactical D&D miniature game, except there are no random card draws and there are no miniatures. Each round players will select two cards from their spell book and use actions to cast spells or maneuver their mages and conjurations around the board. Custom dice are used to determine the outcome of a fight.

Pros:

+ Finely balanced game-play despite the asymmetrical nature of the game.
+ Love the ability to select your available spells each round instead of random card draws.
+ Custom dice are very fast and easy to calculate. Much easier than most complex games with custom dice.
+ The game just makes sense. Everything is very intuitive, despite the overall complexity.
+ Deck construction is very straight forward and more appealing than CCG/LCG counterparts.
+ Great mix of strategy, tactics, and luck.
+ Each mage is has a unique feel.
 
Cons:

- List of keywords are exhaustive, which will have you referencing the rulebook multiple times throughout the game.
- There is a steep learning curve for playing the game. An experience player will easily crush an inexperience player.
- The numerous expansions reinforce the high barrier to entry.
- Generic fantasy along with generic graphic design.
- Enchantment cards are a little fiddly to use.
- Board spaces become overly crowded with summoned creatures.
- Long for a 2 player game, clocking in around 2 hours.
- Playing the game 2 vs 2 degrades the balance and gameplay drastically. It isn't a real option unless you just have to play Mage Wars.

Final Thoughts:

Rating: 9 / 10: Mage Wars is fantastic. Everything is very intuitive, despite the overall complexity. I find that I'm interested in building new spell books, where I never cared for it in games like Magic the Gathering or Android Netrunner. Mage Wars is the type of game that I am thinking about the next day. What went wrong last game? What did I do right? When can this game get back to the table? I highly recommend Mage Wars.
