=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
Wow, this game is really something.  It's a CCG-style game with a substantial tactical combat element that attempts to emulate the feel of dueling mages.  I think it succeeds.  

It is intimidating, in the sense that each player has a "spellbook" (literally a binder full of cards) with dozens of unique cards in at least 6 or 7 major varieties, any one of which often has special power text, and most of which have various special modifier words, which come from a codex of dozens of descriptors.  And, each turn you have access to a huge portion of your spellbook, so the learning curve is a bit steep.  Both of us playing have played many games of this style/complexity before, and in actual play, the complexity wasn't bad, but it's definitely a bit overwhelming.

The game is fun, tactically interesting, and ended up quite even with some interesting exchanges and interactions.  In the end, I think this is a "lifestyle game" though.  The complexity is more of the magnitude of a game like Dungeons & Dragons or Magic: The Gathering, and it seems like it would shine with both players being dedicated to and familiar with the game. I could see this game potentially being very rewarding if one were to make a bit of a "lifestyle" of it.  That said, it was fun even as a one off.

In the end, it's thematic, well-balanced, and extremely complex in a compelling way.

RATING: 7.5

There are certain archetypes that really want to have access to multiple core games. 

However, all the complaints aside, Mage Wars is good fun. It's extremely thematic, has interesting balance, enough depth to last a good time, the expansion have been REALLY good for the game and are what I'd consider "must buy" if you like the game at all. I know I did, at least enough to waste a lot of money on it.

I hope to someday table this a bunch, but I am neither sure as to when that might be, nor with whom.

RATING: 8
Rengeteg kártya. Tényleg.
Sokat játszottunk már vele, kiemelném a pro-t és kontrákat, majd adok egy rövid összegzés.

Pozitívum:
- a téma nagyon jó
- a kártyák és az összes kis tartozék minősége tökéletes
- a játék mechanikája érthető, és nagyon jól működik
- nagyon egyszerű a kipakolás (persze csak miután a varázslókönyv össze lett állítva)
- fordulatos játékmenet, igen erős stratégiával
- nagyon nagyon nagyon kevés random!!!
- az alap dobozzal is rengeteget lehet játszani

Negatívum:
- nagyon nehéz megtanítani a sok kis apró szabály miatt
- vannak olyan helyzetek, ahol egy kártya XY jelölőre hivatkozik, ami nem is létezik. Ilyenkor magadnak raksz rá valamit (legalábbis ezt tanácsolták fórumon), de komolyan... ez egy drága játék, miért nem lehetett belerakni minden jelölőt, ami kellhet?
- Amikor két olyan hero játszik egymás ellen, akik sok kártyával a táblán operálnak, egy idő után átláthatatlan, mi zajlik a táblán. És még a kisebbik baj, mert van, hogy el sem férnek a kártyák a mezőkön, főleg, ha mindenre van rárakva egy kazal enchantment
- azt mondták, ez nem egy LCG fajta, de igazából nagyon is az. Rengeteg pénz kell hozzá, ha követni akarod az összes kiegészítőt

RATING: 8
Oof...this is a huge game.  Excellent at representing the details of a duel of mages, but it has so many rules to keep track of while you are playing.

It is intuitive though.  Everything makes thematic sense, but it is not a game to play casually.  It demands commitment from you and your opponent.  

It also requires intimate deck-building, as it's a must to know every card in your spellbook, so you can plan and react to the opponent's ploys in the most effective way.

Update:  This is a time-demanding game and has a huge learning curve for a 1st player that would most likely be handed an already-made spellbook they know nothing about.  I think it is a fantastic evolution of the fantasy battle genre, but its quirkiness makes it difficult to recruit new players and its length time always becomes a factor.

I backed Allegiance on Kickstarter because it looks like it streamlines the concept allowing for more games on my lifetime.

Update 2:  Allegiance is not the Mage Wars killer I thought it would be.  It plays faster but it fails to capture the thematic overabundance of the more complex Mage Wars.

RATING: 10
Mage Wars has become my favourite two player board game. Its depth and complexity gives us a game that is easy to learn to play but challenging to master.

The appeal comes from the Living Card Game model. No longer will the player have to buy endless boosters to find the Darkfenne Hydra he needs for his book, but instead he has what he needs at his fingertips. The deep customisable element of the game makes for new matches every single time. 

Arcane Wonders themselves have impressed me with their level of customer service, I had bought a game expansion that was unplayable and my problem was solved within 24 hours.

This is not just a game but a hobby, one that I spend many hours doing, and has one of the best games communities I have found, online and face to face. I would recommend this to any Board Game Geek!

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*每人拿角色卡，狀態版，根據角色設定狀態方塊拿對應角色魔法書，擲骰決定誰先
*遊戲輪流行動直到有人HP歸0
*每輪分成準備階段跟行動階段
>>準備階段同時進行
1.交換起始標記，決定行動階段的起始跟所有事件的起始
2.重置所有標記，生物圓柱，快速施法圓柱，準備標記(有圖形面朝上)
3.根據回魔率補重魔法值
4.維持階段，支付需要維持的卡片魔法值，沒得付就棄掉，有些效果結算(燃燒，腐化，重生)，有加號的特徵可疊加，不然就取最大值
5.每人選兩張牌蓋著，如果場上有特殊牌可使用魔法也替它們選牌，該回合沒使用的牌回書
6.場上特殊牌使用你替他選的牌，沒使用回書
>>行動階段，所有生物包括魔法師都可行動一次
*在正式行動階段前後皆有一次快速施法階段，照順位決定是否出牌，有出則圓柱翻面，也可在行動階段的生物行動前後使用，只能出特定卡牌
*行動階段照順位每人使用生物行動一次，輪到你一定要動除非對手生物比你多才可pass直到一樣多
*生物進場時放生物原柱背面在上面，標示為已使用，生物行動時圓柱翻面，並棄掉所有防禦標記(對手可在你翻面時決定是否啟動結界)
*生物行動時要先移動才能做其他行動，行動三選一
1.正常移動動一格(不能對角)，之後可選擇做快速行動包含在動一次(移出或移進有敵人格時不能動第二次)，快速攻擊，防禦，快速施法，特殊快速行動
2.做完整行動，完整攻擊，施法，特殊行動
>>施法步驟 宣告要出的牌>扣魔力>對手宣告是否反擊>結算卡牌
*快速生物可移動兩次在做快速行動，飛行生物移動時忽略牆跟非飛行生物
*結界牌進場是是蓋著的，並支付進場費用，翻開要在支付一次翻開費用，翻開結界不算行動，也可在對手回合做，紅色翻開支付格符合條件一定要開
*裝備牌都只能裝一個，只有戒指可以裝兩個
>>攻擊分成10個步驟 宣告攻擊對象>支付費用(如果有)>昏迷的擲骰看失誤率>擲骰防禦，一次攻擊只能選一種防禦，如果成功跳過攻擊骰階段(有次數的用準備標記記錄)>擲對應數量攻擊骰跟效果骰>結算結果(1代表成功，所以1骰面加起來減防禦代表傷害，1+括號代表強擊，直接扣對應數量血)>額外攻擊(要從昏迷失誤開始重做)>有護盾則反擊傷害(遠距不結算)>防手方有對應特徵可反擊>結束
*有數字的特徵標記可支付魔力移除
*有防禦的生物只要有快速攻擊就可反擊，並可防禦該區的其他物件(敵人要優先打你)，防禦標記下回合該生物行動前移除，被近距攻擊後也移除

RATING: 8
So I'm three games into it so far and I'm liking it! But it's definitely a best-of/worst-of both worlds thing: all the fussiness and precision of a miniatures game, and all the complexity of a CCG-type game.

There's a lot to like here. I like that your full book is available to you and that there's no shuffling or randomness. That's interesting and it's also overwhelming. I like the physicality and dimensionality of the arena. I like the vibe! The spells look and sound like big bad spells, the monsters are interesting. Reminds me a little of the Thunderstone IP.

I do not love that it's so, so fussy. There's no real elegance to any part of the game: it's on you to remember what all needs upkeep, what all gets mana, and so on. There are a million keywords, each with meaningful rules behind them. Three games in and I still haven't memorized the "Codex" of all the little rules and subrules. Lots of subtleties to timing that are gonna be neat once I've figured them out, but will piss me off while I'm figuring them out. 

But there's definitely a lot of game here. I haven't even tried to customize any deck so far. It's all just straight-up starter decks.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Take every single effect from every card game ever made and jam them all into one game. There, that's Mage Wars. A long rule book hides what's in effect a simple game structure. Each round you take turns to action each of your creatures once, where actioning means move/half attack or don't move/full attack. Of course there are a zillion effects that allow you to break this simple paradigm, and that's the fun of the game, to find interesting combos and ways to take advantage of your effects. In effect a monster CCG all in one box, with big box expansions instead of booster packs, which is what the market was crying out for for years and the LCG's have partially tapped. The question is do you want a 2 player card game going for 90-120 minutes or would you gain more satisfaction from playing more games of its baby brother, Summoner Wars. And do you need another 2 player card game at all? I don't because it doesn't provide anything significantly new, just more and more of similiar mechanics we've seen in other games. But it's still a crackingly solid game for those who have room in their collection for such.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This is a game I was very reluctant to start playing. Since I like variation I play a lot of games very seldom, and always like to learn new games which demands that they are farely easy to get started with. This game need you to know a lot of cards to be able to build a powerful spellbook. Yes of course you can play with the prebuild spellbooks, but how fun is that when everyone you play with have their own? 

Anyway, The other week I was more or less forced to play it with a friend and got hooked. 

It's a really great game. I haven't played it more than twice, both times with my friends spellbooks, so I still have to learn to build my own books. 

First try was a two player game which I lost due to rookie mistakes. 

Second try was a four player game with the Forcemaster vs Warlord expansion. We played teams and me and my friend decided to kill the others of one at a time, which was probably quite boring for the guy we took down first, especially since his partner didn't do much to help.

But still, both two-player and two-against-two were really fun to play, so I had to buy my own copy of the game. 

One thing I don't like is the pricing of expansions. 
if you want it all why buy the two tomes, extra player tokens and two more spellbooks when that is 20-25% more expensive than buying another full core game. The only thing you don't get that way are six duplicate cards: 

Hand of Bim-Shalla,  Jinx, Regrowth Belt, Shift Enchantment and two copies of Purify

and instead of four player colors you get double of red and blue, but a little paint will change that.

Instead you get another 110 cards, double all the tokens, two more playerboards and another gameboard.

RATING: 7.5
It's pretty good! There's a lot of deep thinking involved, but it often goes to waste. Keep it simple and make some big plays.

One of the downsides is that the dice rolling mechanic leaves a lot of the game up to luck. Fortunately, the rolls are fairly predictable and you can compensate by making safe plays for important targets and risky plays when it matters less.

They could probably make this game better just by doubling the damage and life of everything, thus halving the randomness.

Incidentally, this game probably has one of the best rule books ever considering how complex it is.

RATING: 8
My initial play in apprentice mode did not go well for me.  I ran my fully equipped Warlock right into a couple of Tanglevines cast by the insidious Beastmaster, and proceeded to self-nuke myself into oblivion with my Firestorm spell.  

And I loved every minute of it.  It's a little heavy on iconography and keywords, but I found the gameplay tense and compelling.  I imagine the learning curve can be steep for non-gamers, but me and my best friend (both fairly serious gamers) sorted through a full game in around an hour, with minimal rule book checking. 

If I had a complaint, it would be that the core box should come with four spell books and action markers.  I know it would increase the cost of entry, and would likely not be used by the majority of players...HOWEVER, not including them turns this game into a barely tolerable $60 ($45 online) two player game, which is pricey, and probably has scared off a few players.  

But, here's the skinny from my perspective: I really, really like this game, and want to play it more.  My biggest fear is that WANTING to play it might be all that comes from my purchase, rather than actually GETTING to play it.  

Update:  Have now played a second game and am beginning to wonder if the Beastmaster can be beaten.  

Update 2:  Gave it some thought and dropped half a point.  The set up time is prohibitive and although I enjoy it, I can't say I sit around hoping someone asks to play it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]RO:[/b] [i]03/09/2016[/i]

I've finally got enough plays of this in to actually form an opinion of it, thanks to a guy from my group that enjoys this sort of game as much as I do. My initial impressions of Mage Wars (back when it just known as Mage Wars, sans Arena) were very positive. Epic (and I mean this literally, games can last hours) duels between mages, variable player powers, and your very own customizable spellbook where all of your powers are available to you at any time (because really, that makes a helluva lot more sense than drawing your abilities blind from a deck). What's not to love? Really, not much. I have very little to complain about here besides the length and how awkward spellbook creation (which I hear is ameliorated by an app for this express purpose) and resetting can be (you have to put each of the cards back into the sleeves in your spellbook after a game). Plus, it is very fiddly, flipping over activation markers, remembering to channel on familiars and spawnpoints, adding and removing tokens for damage, mana, status effects, etc. but with that said it all works together very well and once you know what you're doing it is an absolute blast.

Luck is a significant factor in game, you will sometimes be chucking seven or eight dice for an attack and if the dice aren't kind you may find yourself in a very precarious situation, very quickly. This is somewhat alleviated by you being able to pull any of your many tricks out of your hat more-or-less whenever you want. You ready two cards from your spellbook per turn (more if you have a spawnpoint or familiar) and you have to try to anticipate and respond to what you think your opponent is going to do while trying to work your own strategy. Balance keeping creatures on the board, buffing them, debuffing your opponent, and slapping gear on yourself, and your mage is no defenseless slouch him/herself when kitted out in an array of gear; which isn't a bad idea since the goal of the game is to kill your opponent's mage.

There is a very intense and satisfying back and forth. It might [i]look[/i] like you're down and out but with clever play and bit of luck you're right back in it and pressing your advantage. It reminds me of Summoner Wars (yes, I know people hate that comparison, but it's apt) but is far, far more interesting and deep.

Overall this is a fantastic game, I didn't get to play it almost at all for years after my impulsive purchase but this one has legs and I look forward to many plays in the future with this fascinating and engaging system. I'm going to bump the rating for this up to an eight.

Once understanding all keywords in the codex has been mastered, then next level seems to be experimenting with creating spellbooks and learning the nuances of each mage.  There seems to be a ton of replayability here and with expansions coming, that potential just increases.  My 8 year old son is loving this and I think this is going to be our go-to game.

RATING: 9
Unangefochten meine Nr. 1 der Neuerscheinungen im Jahr 2012 und auch das Spiel, das mich in den vergangenen zweieinhalb Monaten vielleicht nicht ununterbrochen, so doch immer wieder beschäftigt hat. Vorweg: Ich war bisher nie ein großer ‚Deckbuilder‘ vor dem Herrn, zumal ich wenig Interesse (und Geduld) habe, zufällige Synergien (oder deren Ausbleiben) in einer konkreten Spielsituationen auszuprobieren. Eben dies aber ist bei „Mage Wars“ auch gar nicht nötig: Es gibt keinen Zufall mit Blick auf das, was ein Spieler aktiv tun kann (wie bei „Magic: The Gathering“); der Zufall kommt allenfalls bei der Effektivität der Zaubersprüche qua Würfel ins Spiel (und an dieser Effektivität kann man ‚schrauben‘). Auch die Befürchtung, die Kontrahenten würden angesichts der Tatsache, daß sie stets alle ihre Sprüche verfügbar haben, zu lang nachdenken, erwies sich als unbegründet: In den meisten Situationen existiert nur eine überschaubare Anzahl Alternativen. Jedenfalls hat „Mage Wars“ mit seinen durchsichtigen, logischen und - soweit ich sehe – widerspruchsfreien Regeln mich auch jenseits des Spieltisches immer wieder zum Nachdenken veranlaßt: Wie wäre ein Priestess-Build mit im wesentlichen einer starken, aber ‚dunklen‘ Kreatur? Oder ein Wizard-Build, der ganz auf Conjurations setzt? Zwei neue Mages sind als Erweiterung bereits für Anfang 2013 angekündigt. - Absolut empfehlenwert.

RATING: 10
I've owned this game for some time and have all the expansions. So I feel I'm able to give a fair review to this.

I love this game. The theme is fabulous, the art work is generally amazing. There's a lot of thought gone into the mechanics. While there is a bit of reading in the manual, it is explained very well and covers cases in detail. This is a deep strategy game that will generally play from 45-120 minutes, depending on the players/mages chosen. (It does say 90 minutes playtime on the box, so I really can't understand people giving a low score because it takes this long to play). The idea of a spellbook where you start the round having the choice from every single one of your spells available is so simple that it's almost revolutionary. 

It's so much fun and the rounds and actions follow how I'd imagine a battle between mages would happen. Like a spell thrown at another creature will hit unless it has some kind of defense, holy healing spells making creatures gain health, evil curses hindering and hurting. Fantastic creatures mixed with commonplace ones will all rise to the defense of the mage as he commands. The variety is there too - if I want an evil Warlock with holy Angels fighting for me, I can have that. Likewise a holy mage summoning demons. You pay extra spellbook costs, but that's the only limitation to building your own deck!

Rather than give a full review here, I'll just add that I think this game is pretty close to perfect for me. I love it, as do all the people I've introduced it to in my playgroup. It's one of the games I never turn down an option to play.

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
A very good game indeed. It can really conjure up the image of dueling mages. 
I like that there are many ways to play whether you like to summon a horde of undead monsters or you prefer a nearly invincible warrior clad in iron or perhaps like to teleport around the arena attacking your enemy where he is weakest with fireballs and lightning strikes, the tools are there - at least if you buy the expansions.

There is some luck in the dice, but since it will on average take more than 6 dice to kill an unprotected mage - and much more if he wears armour, the luck tends to even out quite rapidly. Also, there are ways to mitigate the luck, like curses that are not dependent upon dice or choosing the right target for your attack.

All in all there is a lot of direct interaction and a lot of decission making. It is also a bit of a life-style game in that it does require frequent plays and to get the most out of it one will also need to spend time on it while not playing.

A friend and I can usually get two games in of this during a single evening - after the kids have been put to bed but before we ourselves need to turn in. So slightly more than 1 hour per game. And those few games that last longer are usually all the more exciting for it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
20-01-2014. Apparently I never commented on MageWars (MW), but I have graded it.

MW is a game in which everyone selects a summoner, builds (or in my case takes) a spellbook. You then proceed to gather resources, cast spells, maneuver tactically on the board while bombarding your opponent with spells and creatures. 
Interesting twist on the Summoner versus Summoner theme, by having you select cards from your spellbook eliminating the luck of the draw.

I really, really enjoy the game. For me, the biggest downside is the duration of the game. I haven't played games that lasted under 90 minutes and most have lasted 120 to 150 minutes. For a two-player game, how challenging and interesting as it might be.. that makes it hard to get to the table.

MtG/ StarWars and Android Netrunner play in 20-30 minutes per game. This is ideal for a 2 player game. MageWars is the epic variant, and while I love the game I cannot whip it out as often as I'd like.

RATING: 6
[Edited this review to reflect MW: Arena without expansions]

Pros: Better production values and cost compared to FFG's LCGs. Extensive deck building possibilities. Terrific artwork.

Cons: The highly vaunted spell book mechanic and flat resource curve are as much as a bug as they are a feature. Leads to repetitive and exhaustingly long games. No hand management or meta skill; just a game of trading nukes (some cards are literally hard counters to others).  Spatial elements are not utilized well and clunky (Domination fixes this issue a bit). No good mitigation of the actual RNG element (dice, which are required to offset the lack of card variance) outside of rerolls which are terrible. 

Basically a game that tries to be too many things and is beloved by terrible TCG players who blame their failures on card draw, resource screw, and inability to manage their hand in-game. Sorry, but complexity does not equal depth.

RATING: 8.2
positives:

Two elements of the game are unique and never find it in other games i've played.
First of all , you dont have to wait to draw the card you want like all other games, you just simply pay (if you have mana) and take it in your hand! 
Secondly you build your own spellbook (you have a limit amound of money 120) just think how deep is that, if something doesnt go right for you in the battle, you can modify your spellbook in the next game! 

The game has very deep strategic desicions as you choose in most cases only 2 cards each round of your spellbook.

Negatives:
The serious drawback of the game is that the game is overloaded with many many rules and codex, and this has serious impact on the game duration, which definetely is not 90 minutes, about 180 minutes is usually the game time and sometimes higher, and secondly there is a change you miss something to see in the rules or forget it at that time and suddenly your strategy goes in the trash! 

It's a great game but needs dedicated players :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is the coolest idea for an LCG I've ever heard of. I like the idea of having access to your spells whenever you want, and having to pick them at the start of the turn, before seeing what your opponent chooses, adds just the right amount of uncertainty. I like the uncertainty added by the damage dice and effect die, and I rarely feel like a game is determined by the dice. This from someone that usually dislikes dice combat resolution systems. I also appreciate the different mage abilities and schools of magic. It adds a nice amount of asymmetry to the game even before taking into account your individual spell choices. 

----------------------

Mage Wars is an incredible game, but there are a few problems with it.

The two biggest problems are the barrier to entry and the card distribution. There is a bit of a learning cliff to Mage Wars. I expect it to take about 30 games to really learn the game well enough to be playing competently. If you can play the game in 30-90 minutes, that's still a fairly significant time investment, but it gets a lot worse if you play slowly. There are plenty of people that cite 4 hour game times. Once you know what you're doing, you should be able to play 3+ games in that amount of time, but particularly if you don't have an experienced player teaching the game, the first few games can be quite long. This makes teaching new players, and finding new players willing to commit to learning the system extremely challenging and tedious if you don't already have a community built up around the game.

The card distribution in the core set is kind of awful. Later expansions do a better job of it, but it still suffers from a distribution model standpoint. Because Mage Wars has adopted an LCG style model, the game is becoming progressively more expensive to get into, which makes it less and less attractive to new players just from a cost standpoint. In addition, there are some cards that are incredibly important, and you'll want to run in virtually every spellbook (Acid Ball), and others that are borderline useless (Sardonyx). For those that want to be able to run more than one spellbook, there is no good, efficient way to get enough copies of the cards that they actually need multiple copies of without getting piles of cards they'll never use. 

All of that said, Mage Wars is an incredible game if you can find someone to play it with on a regular basis.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
1 play, prototype, demo game, Origins 2011

The basic concept reminded me of Summoner Wars (which I'd gotten a partial demo of last year, but never played otherwise; but I just traded for a copy of all previous releases -- should be winging its way to me right now):
- each player is a Mage (Summoner) of a different type
- you play on a board with your character represented by a card
- you summon other cards to the board
- everything moves and attacks each other
- you win by killing the other player's main character

Of course, much of the above is true for other games as well. There are also many differences between these games (at least, what I vaguely remember about Summoner Wars). And they were just demoing a very basic version of the game -- there is apparently a lot more to it! Some differences:
- board shows zones (intro game is a 3x2 grid), not individual spaces; movement and spell effects is by zone
- bigger variety of available cards: creatures, spells, enchantments, equipment
- here, you have your entire spell book (a physical book, with slots for all your spell cards) from which to choose each turn; at the start of each turn, select 2 to have available that turn; uncast ones go back to spell book while the cast ones are discarded
- everything on the board acts each turn

Mage Wars felt sort of like a miniatures-type combat game, in the Magic: the Gathering type of universe and played with cards! Or like a combat real time video game brought to the board: you're in an arena, you earn mana at a steady rate, you get to choose what to cast each turn rather than depending on the luck of the draw, all your monsters are moving around and acting at the same time, yet you don't have to worry about some minutiae (like exact movement, ranges, line of sight) thanks to the zone system.

The demo game was already set up -- specific main characters and spellbooks -- but I suspect you could build your own spellbook (maybe to a specific total point value) ... great for the CCG player in you! Also, the board was a simple 3x2 grid; there's room to change or expand the play area and add terrain effects as well. And, of course, there can be a ton of variety in creature strengths and abilities. The game has huge potential!

I am not a particularly good tactician; in fact, I think I'm downright awful! But I still had a lot of fun with this one!

--- Summary (prototype, demo game) ---
Each player picks a different starting character and matching spellbook. Place the card in opposite corners of the board. These show how much mana you generate each turn, and your characteristics (cast a spell, attack dice, HP). Random player gets initiative marker: holder goes first.

Each round, players simultaneously untap (actually, you flip a marker) all their creatures. Add mana to your mana pool. Resolve Upkeep effects, if any. Choose which 2 spells to place in your hand this turn. Then, players alternately activate a creature on the board to use its ability (move -- most move 1 zone, attack ranged or melee, possibly other abilities); that taps (inactivates) the creature. Players also have the opportunity to take one action (fast spell) in addition to, or instead of, one of their other actions that round. If you pass, you're out for the rest of the round. When combat occurs, roll the specified number of dice: these show 0-2 hits, some with a starburst -- the targets shields/armor are deducted from the non-starburst hits, then the sum is inflicted onto the target. Some hits inflict additional effects (e.g., possibility of setting target on fire!). Continue this way until one player has taken maximum damage and loses!

Basic rules are nice and simple -- the difficulty comes from the tactical choices to be made. It's hard for me to give a good evaluation since I suck so badly at these games, but I'd love to try this again (before next year!). 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I adore this game.  I knew I would before I even got my copy and was even more so sure before I ever played it just as I was reading through the rules and card text.  As a former Magic: The Gathering and other assorted CCG lover I go absolutely giddy for this kind of stuff.  But finally playing it has put the nail in the coffin, this game is outstanding!

Many other players have compared this game to Summoner Wars and that is a fair baseline but I am head over heals for Mage Wars and only lukewarm toward Summoner Wars.  Both games feature playing cards onto a map of sorts attempting to destroy the other player's "leader" and feature a similar combat system.  But, as the saying goes, the devil is in the details.

I am all about depth and variety in my games, especially ones I purchase and Mage Wars blew me out of the water in this respect, especially when compared to Summoner Wars.  With just 4 different mages (and 2 more on the way in an imminent expansion) the customizability of your spell book gives a player almost unlimited options.  Each player has a certain number of "points" to spend on their spell book and pays more for spells outside of their magical "focus" but aside from these restrictions (and a couple spells that are mage-specific) you can put ANY spell in the game in your spell book.  This means you can play the same two mages against each other countless times and never feel like two games are the same.  Certainly, some mages excel at certain strategies and tactics but just having that kind of control over your deck is very attractive to me.

I've always been a little disappointed by the LCG's available coming from a background of Magic since the depth of the games seems lack-luster compared to the mechanic-rich 20-year history of Magic.  Mage Wars is the first that doesn't feel like it pales too much in comparison though.  It's certainly no Magic: the Gathering but there is a glossary of different mechanics and conditions that form a nice cohesive web that works well together and presents a good number of strategies, combos, and play-styles out-of-box.

I also love the mechanic, the first I've played in this way, of giving you access to your entire deck library from the start of the game.  There is no luck of the draw, just management of your resources, actions, and board position.  This can make the game overwhelming to new players who don't know yet the different cards and how they interact with one another and that is probably my biggest (and only) complaint with the game.  It has a steep learning curve if only because of the things I love most about the game (its depth and variety).

This is one of my new favorite games and I will be looking to play it as often as possible.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
First play (pre-made Beastmaster vs Warlock): Played a couple of rules wrong but it still was a great game. Had to explain first (took 1 hour to do properly) and abandoned after 2 hours due to time limitations but we both were wanting to go on.

Second play (pre-made Wizard vs Priest): We abandoned the game after a 3 hour matchup, having only 6 damage my Wizard and almost none on the Priest. While playing it was very enjoyable, it was thoroughly unsatisfying to leave the game unfinished. We also recognized the game would not go anywhere for at least another hour so stopping was in itself not that bad. Used a tactic of extreme turtling, btw.

Third play: (pre-made Warlock vs Priest): Used different playstyle, a bit more aggressive and BAM! What a great game. Clocked in at 1hr45min with my Warlock only 1 life remaining. All came down to the last dice-roll.

So, this game is all about how you play. As a 'blue' MTG player, I used to focus on the meta-game, controlling that first and then slowly killing my opponent off. Fun for me, not that fun for my opponents sometimes. In this game, it is paramount you put pressure on the other guy, and do it better than he can counter it. Gameplay hugely improves after some games as you better understand the flow of the game.

Don't be afraid to put down a big creature early. Big creatures don't go down as fast as in MtG (no Terror/Sword to Plowshares or other similars here). And don't be afraid to use those direct damage spells early on.

The main risk by which this game falls flat for 'new' players is usually not in the complexity of the rules (though yes, they can be daunting at first), nor the play-experience itself (which is great) but in the duration of this experience.

Suggested for first play: Pre-made Apprentice Warlock vs Apprentice Beastmaster (see Arcane Wonders website). Both builds are quick, heavy hitters that will get the action going. Priestess, while very good, is mainly making a game longer with all her heals, and Wizard, while perhaps being my favorite build, also does that by choking the other's mana.

So get a 'quick' game going and only gradually jump in. Don't expect that 'cause you are an experienced boardgamer' you'll master this game within a first few plays. If you like MtG and/or Summoner Wars: buy it. And give yourself a proper learning experience.

Final note: in this game it is paramount to try whacky stuff and play aggressive. Sure, you might lose. But consider this:

As the goal of playing a game is having fun...
Don't win the game and lose the meta-game of having fun.

[EDIT SEP 2014] Bought a second base-set to accomodate my builds without having the tedious switch-over of the basics (dissolves, dispels, nullifies). It is just that great. Re-rated to a perfect 10.

RATING: 10
Mage Wars is a revelation for all lovers of demanding and complex games, filled with tactical tricks and elaborated maneuvers. Its battle for life and death inside an arena filled with magic. Every player conjures up lots of servants during this fight. They will fight for him, but finally battle reaches wizards themselves. And one of them must fall. Do not let enemy catch you off guard and always be ready. This is your motto, whenever you step inside the arena. You can equip yourself with all kinds of defensive spells, guardians or enchantments, which can ultimately lead to victory. But you need large amout of mana to do this and it comes in limited numbers. And although you have lots and lots of tricks inside your spellbook, you have time to do only up to two spells each round, one of them must be easy to cast and you read incantation for whole turn, so your mage cannot move at all. Both wizards create their spellbooks themselves before the game according to class, they are trained in. No lack of variability is even possible. Game has only a little shortcomings, when it comes to processing - missing blue cubes (you get black instead) and rotated pages for spell cards in second part of the book. But then you reach difficulty and though base mechanics are really easy, straightforward and revolve around activating of characters and only two special quick action phases, there are tens of keywords standing in your way. Some cards are present more than once inside the game, but otherwise each card is a unique creation and does things a little differently. So you have to know your spellbook to be a good mage. But that is not a bad thing, right? There are incredible tactical choices, filled with traps, defenses, flying, ranged attacks, teleportation and others. Its unbeatable problem for inexperienced and children under 12 years. But if you are trained in board games, you will be thrilled. Mage Wars are the boy dream comes true for every player, who likes fantasy theme. And you can now fulfil your dream! All it takes is one journey to a closest retailer shop! 

Whole review:
http://www.deskovehry.com/s-pribehem/976/review-mage-wars-captivating-duel-of-two-wizards/en

RATING: 3
I tried to make it work, but this game is just not good. Way too many keywords and way too long. There is literally an entire codex of keywords. They keywords themselves aren't the worst problem. The issue is that some of the words are understood to encompass other keywords, so you have all of these nested keywords that you either need to memorize or you have to constantly look up while learning the game.

Then there's the length. While learning the game it can easily go 3-4 hours. Once you know it then it will still come in at 2-3 hours. This is just too long for the experience.

Summoner Wars is vastly superior. Sure, there's a ton more stuff going on in Mage Wars, but that certainly doesn't make it better.

Sold.

RATING: 6
2013-11-29 

I'm really on the fence about this one. Rating may change after subsequent plays. 

"Hey guys, we'll fix Magic: The Gathering by eliminating the luck of the draw! Oh wait, we'll also make all combat reliant on dice rolls, so that an attack that rolls four dice could do anywhere from zero to eight damage! Yup, we sure fixed that randomness factor!"

OK. Magic: The Gathering front-loads randomness in the form of a shuffled deck, while Mage Wars relies on random dice throws to determine combat damage. Who am I to say which is better? 

Well, for one, Magic: The Gathering is chock-full of ways to mitigate its particular form of randomness. A properly built MtG deck will have multiple, redundant ways in which to achieve its strategic goal. It will also have card draw spells, card selection spells, and tutoring spells to ensure that it gets the cards it needs at the right time. If I'm playing a deck that relies on getting big creatures out quickly to directly attack my opponent, I'll run cards like Rampant Growth that allow me to get lands out of my deck and directly into play. If I'm playing a deck that relies on a insta-win combo, I'll run cards like Demonic Tutor that allow me to get the essential combo pieces out of my deck and into my hand. And you can bet that I'll have multiple copies of essential cards to ensure that I draw into them. 

In Mage Wars, if I roll poorly, what can I do? Jack squat, that's what. All those Analysis Paralysis - riddled choices I made selecting spells from my spell book of dozens of cards come down to a crapshoot. 

If my attack doesn't go through in M:tG, it's because my opponent interacted with me, not because I got screwed by dice.

It also takes far too long and requires too much time investment outside of the game for what it is.

2014-1-20

Decided to give this game one more shot. We'll see this Tuesday.

RATING: 10
I've had this game on my radar for a long time, but kept dismissing it for seeming like another Magic clone.
Well, was I wrong not picking this up earlier! Already the first game I played completely blew me away.
Sure it feels like Magic in theme, but gets rid of everything I didnt like about it.
First and foremost, the luck-of-the-draw gets completely pushed out with the spellbook. Having all spells available to you right from the start is a real breath of fresh air, and creates a sense of true freedom in tactics and strategy, not to mention a ton of immersion. (luck is still a factor though, but this time in the form of dice)
Although the spells induce plenty of different effects and conditions, resulting in a extensive terminology, most things appear very intuitive to anybody who is a little bit acquainted with fantasy and rpgs. (and common sense for that matter)

RATING: 6
I've only played the demo version of this, since the actual retail release is still a ways off. The game is basically a riff on Magic the Gathering, the twist being that every spell in your deck is available to you at all times. Combat also has a spatial aspect, as the game takes place over multiple zones in which your mage and summoned creatures are free to roam. Think Summoner Wars, but with far more intricate play. Overall, I'd say the game has a lot of potential. There are aspects of it that I wish were a bit less complicated, but as I mentioned before, the game is still in development. The design team seemed pretty open to feedback at my demo, so there is a very good chance that some of the game's rough edges will get cleaned up before release day. 

Definitely one to keep an eye on.

RATING: 3
+Tons of cool options
+Solid theme and cool art
+Very affordable for a miniatures style game
+Makes you feel like an awesome grand mage
-Slow ramp up (First few turns always seem to be just building up your side of the board)
-Far too much luck and variance in the dice rolls
-Takes forever to end the game
-Too AP prone
-Setting up the spellbook can take a long time and be tedious (Having to count up all the cards based on their level and type)
-Heavy learning curve because of the massive amount of mechanics (Cards have no explanations and no reference sheets. Where did I leave that rulebook again?)
-Box is ridiculously huge (New version is better)

I can see why some people would like Mage Wars, but it is not for me. Will be getting rid of this one at some point.

RATING: 8
Very well done Magic dueling game. It really feels like two mages battling an epic duel. The sides are very diverse. Very interesting mechanics are that you can select every spell in a book from the start of the game. 
It's tactical : you move your creatures and mage in an arena where positioning is important. Strategical : you'll need a long term plan with your mage to win. 

Minor nitpicks: 

* you need to deckbuild because the starting spellbooks are unbalanced
* you'll need a few games before you will be comfortable with the rules
* first game will be daunting and overwhelming : a lot of cards to read  
* requires both players to be equally skilled
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is what I wish Magic the Gathering was. It is an epic customizable card game providing you with the most thematic feeling of dueling mages ever devised. It does take a lot longer to play (90 minutes) and is less portable, so it doesn't fit the same niche as Magic does as a portable filler, but man the experience is amazing.

The game is almost pure strategy since you don't have to worry about random card draws. Yes, there are dice, but they have a very predictable bell curve and they provide randomness of outcome rather than randomness of choice. You can make plans around the dice, and you can make contingency plans in case things don't go as you wished. That's not something you can do with random card draws where your decisions are limited by what's in your hand. Having your entire spell book available at all times makes this game incredibly strategic, and having the board gives you tactical positioning as well.

I just can't stress enough how much fun this game is and how much it rewards good strategic planning. It truly is a battle of wits with fireballs. The only downside I can think of is the learning curve. It's not really any worse than other CCG's or Miniature games though, so if you're comfortable with those you should be fine. Still, there are a lot of rules and card interactions to digest and it will take even an experienced gamer many plays before they get everything right.

RATING: 9.5
被不少人誉为版图实体化后的万智牌，当对规则及卡牌效果都熟悉后，游戏犹如突然从抽象概念转变成具体活物，规则框架极为严谨也相对繁琐。此外目前基础版仅是这个法师世界的冰山一角，从此雏形上就可以看到其无穷的扩张力和源源不断的生命力。虽然此类游戏的运气因素大些，但天命人不可违，你需要作出的即你对现场情况的最佳判断。。。
【关键词】魔法书，所属派系，自由出牌，快速施法，以太/屏障/保护/反击/飞行/无生命/墙，负面状态，装备，特制攻击骰子，12面效果骰

RATING: 8
A really very good customisable game in the vein of Magic. Removes the random drawing from a deck and gives you access to your whole deck - spellbook - from the get go. Adds a board and some random dice for combat.

All in all very good, but drops a bit for game length and a steep learning curve. But persevere, it's worth it.
